course, like our friend Jim, I can cite logical “reasons” for this choice, chief of which is that for
spreads I eliminate all pip cards (2's through l0's) in the four suits, leaving only 42 cards in the
deck. So it seemed to me important to find a spread that uses a proportionately small number of
cards. The reason I eliminated the pips is because these cards as they appear in the Marseilles
deck are relatively uninteresting. They can offer us only the symbolism of their numbers which is
already available in the twenty-two Trumps.
If you have never before spread the cards, you might begin with the Nine Card Oracle. If this
one doesn't feel right for you, then I suggest that you look at other Tarot books in the public library
until you find a spread you like. There are, it appears, about as many systems for laying out the
cards as there are books on the subject. To my way of thinking, choosing which pattern you will
use is exactly as important (or unimportant) as deciding which clothes you will wear to consult the
cards. The important thing is to find something that fits and feels comfortable.

--- Page 280 ---
THE NINE-CARD TAROT ORACLE
METHOD 1: OPEN FACE SPREAD
(NOTE: This spread is useful only if you are 
unfamiliar
 with the Tarot. If you are already
acquainted with the symbolism of the cards, you will find Method 2 more helpful.)
Place the cards 
face up
 on a large flat surface. If you can't find a table big enough, use the
floor.
Pick out the nine cards that interest you most or catch your imagination. Choose them carefully,
but not intellectually. Don't try to decipher a card's title or decode its symbolism. Respond
spontaneously and emotionally. Does the picture intrigue you? Does it strike a responsive cord in
you?
In choosing these nine cards, it's important to remember that there is no such thing as a “bad”
card. As with the sixty-four hexagrams of the I Ching, each belongs to its time and season. Also,
as with the I Ching, their meaning is symbolic rather than literal – so that one might choose, say,
Death or The Hanged Man with no implication that this “means” literal death, physical torture,
suicide, etc.

--- Page 281 ---
Having chosen your nine cards, put away the rest, leaving the cards you have selected face up.
Now study the cards carefully and choose from their number the one that seems closest to your
idea of yourself 
at the present moment.
 This will be known hereafter as The Card of You (position
#1 in diagram). In choosing it, remember that this card represents your self-image at this moment
in time. You are not stuck with this selection forever. Next month – or even next week – you might
choose another card.
Having selected The Card of You, leave it face up on your table and collect the remaining eight
cards. Keeping them face down, shuffle them. While you are shuffling, make a wish or ask the
Tarot a question – preferably about a matter that is pending. 
The moment you have found your
wish or question, stop shuffling.
 Cut the cards and complete your cut.
Now deal out four of these eight cards and set them aside in a pile, 
face down.
 These are the
Oracle Cards that speak specifically to your wish or question. They will be discussed later.
Since the exact wording of your wish or question is important, it's a good idea to stop here and
write this down exactly as you have worded it to yourself. Remember that the Tarot, unlike the
Ouija Board, has no way of responding with an unequivocal “Yes” or “No” answer. For this reason,
it's best to word your wish or question so that it doesn't require a definite answer. It is helpful if
you can introduce your queries with such phrases as: “What can you tell me about . . . . ?” Or,
“What am I overlooking in . . . (this situation)?” Or “Will you please amplify . . . . ?”
At this point you have The Card of You face up in the center of the table, the four Oracle Cards
face down in a pile at one side, and the four 
remaining cards still in one hand. When you have
written down your wish or question, it is time to deal out these four cards. Place them face up and
clockwise, as shown in the accompanying diagram, starting with Card #6 to the left of The Card of
You.
Now you will have a picture-story on the table before you, with The Card of You completely
framed by cards #6, #7, #8, and #9, as in the diagram. Study this picture first for a 
general
impression
. (Favorable? Unfavorable? Pleasing? Displeasing?)
Note any recurring pattern that strikes you as significant
 (Repetition of symbols? Alternating Yin
and Yang rhythms? Emphasis on certain colors, shapes, or bodily movements?)
Next study each card individually in order, beginning with The Card of You. Ask yourself these
kinds of questions about each: what first attracted me to this card? In what way might it “belong”
to me? Does it remind me of an incident in my life? Of a person or situation? Jot down any
findings for future reference.
As you move clockwise from card #6 (Recent Past) to card #7 (Present), and card #8
(Immediate Future), and card #9 (Year to Come), contrast each card with the preceding one. What
similarities and/ or differences in tone, flavor, color, mood, and action can you find? Which cards
face each other? Which seem to reject, or turn away from others? Can you discover any “plot” or
progress in the cards as they appear in numerical sequence?
Look especially at card #9 (The Year to Come). Does its action seem to be a gradual
culmination of events pictured in the other cards? Or does it seem quite different? If so, in what
way different?
The five cards we have just been considering speak to your life situation generally. Of course,
they will necessarily also throw light on your wish or question, but less directly so than the four
Oracle Cards. These will speak specifically to your hopes and dreams.
Now it is time to consult The Oracle. Turn up the top card (#5) first. This is the Signifier – so-
called because it is the most significant card of the four. It deals directly with your wish or problem.
What is your first reaction to the Signifier? (Favorable? Unfavorable?) Next, turn up the other three
Oracle Cards. These represent influences at work in connection with your wish or question. Pause
here to catch first reactions to these Influence Cards.
If you made a wish and the Signifier seems to you strongly positive, then your wish probably
has a good chance of being realized – provided that you take into consideration the influences,
personalities and/ or inner tendencies portrayed in the three Influence Cards. Should the Signifier
seem to you strongly negative, then perhaps your wish is not yet ripe for maturation in reality, in

--- Page 282 ---
which case the Influence Cards may offer a clue as to the forces within and without that need first
to be overcome or utilized in this situation.
If you asked a question, the Signifier provides either a direct answer or a veiled clue, and the
Influence Cards give additional details or more clues. In deciding the cards' meaning, your own
intuition is the best key.
Now study all nine cards together as part of one drama. What connection can you find between
the two groups of cards? Pay special attention to The Card of You and the card representing The
Year to Come. Are they facing the Oracle Cards, or are they looking in another direction? How
might the postures, actions, or atmospheres of these two cards affect whatever answer the Oracle
might have given?
METHOD 2: CLOSED FACE SPREAD
In this spread the cards are dealt out in exactly the same order and placed according to the
same diagram as above. The only difference between the two spreads is that in this one 
you do
not choose the cards for your spread.
After shuffling whatever deck you use, you place the cards in one pile 
face down
 and write out
your wish or question. Now cut the cards. Next turn up the top card of this deck and place it face
up in the center of the table in position # 1 as before. After dealing out the four Oracle Cards and
leaving them in one pile face down, you next deal out cards #6, #7, #8, and #9 and place them
face up according to the diagram.
Now proceed to study the cards exactly as described above, jotting down any ideas or
associations for future reference.
SUGGESTIONS: Whichever spread you use, be sure to make a chart of it so that (in perhaps a
week or two) you can lay out the cards once more in the same pattern. In the meantime keep their
pictures and drama in mind and meditate on these from time to time.
Be on the alert for photographs, news clippings, personalities, emotional reactions, or anything
else which seems to connect in any way with the cards that turned up in your spread. You may be
surprised at the flashes of insight that come to you at odd moments when the Tarot is seemingly
farthest from your conscious thoughts.
If you record your dreams, look there also for characters or incidents that might connect with
“your” cards. Jot down even the most insignificant-seeming connections; often together they'll
create a meaningful pattern. Keep a record of the next spread you create and see if you can find
connections between the present spread and the new one. Did you, for example, draw any of the
same cards in the second spread? If so, did some of them fall in the same places as before? If so
(or if not) what conclusions might you draw from whatever turns up?
As I write these words, the Tarot Oracle whispers that this book which we have been writing
together has come to 
its
 conclusion. We wish the reader Godspeed on his Tarot journey. May the
cards bring him good fortune!

--- Page 283 ---
Glossary
by Mary K. Greer
Active Imagination
 (see Creative Imagination).
Affect
—A strong emotional reaction marked by active consequences, such as physical symptoms and
disturbances in thinking. The essential basis of our personality is affectivity. Thought and action
are, as it were, only symptoms of affectivity.
Alchemy
—the forerunner of chemistry, based on the supposed transformation of matter into gold or
into a universal elixir. Psychologically it equates the transformations taking place in chemical
retorts with transformations within our own psychic nature, liberating the spirit that is concealed or
imprisoned in matter.
Amplification
—a method of exploring symbols by analogy and contrast, expanding on associations
and understanding around the central image. It involves gathering all available knowledge on a
particular symbol from personal and impersonal associations.
Analysis
—Jungian therapy that, with the help of a Jungian analyst, aims to achieve a readjustment of
psychological attitude, by bringing the unconscious to consciousness, often through a focus on
neurosis.
Anima/Animus
—terms used by Jungians to indicate the unconscious or concealed female element in
the male and male element in the female, respectively. While operating somewhat differently in
male and female, their basic function is to inspire. Neo-Jungians focus more on both an inner
masculine and feminine within each person.
Archetypes
—universal, innate, inherited and emotionally charged themes that are remnants of
instinctual patterns of meaning in the human psyche that influence our psychology. Archetypes
appear cross-culturally and across time in art, literature and myth as well as in the dreams of
individuals. They are instinctual forces operating autonomously in the depths of the human
psyche in much the same way as the instincts function in the body.
Collective unconscious
—a universal, impersonal and hereditary aspect of the unconscious mind that
is shared by all people. Its primary structures are archetypes, and it contains all those elements
that are necessary for the self-regulation of the psyche as a whole.
Compensation
—the function of completing and balancing consciousness by the unconscious in the
direction of wholeness. It is an expression of the psyche's capacity for self-regulation.
Complex
—a center of energy from related but split off psychic fragments that build up around
emotions. A complex is usually a result of a collision with something in the environment that we
were not prepared to deal with. It contains creative potentials that, when unrecognized, can inhibit
personal development.
Compulsion
—an irresistible urge. In Jungian psychology it often means to unconsciously act in a
manner associated with an archetype or as an expression of a neurosis, but Jung also believed in
a compulsion to self-realization.
Conscious(ness)
—knowledge and awareness of the self and environment through both rational and
irrational sensory and mental processes.
Constellate/Constellation(s)
—the coming together of elements, images and complexes in the
unconscious to form a consciously recognizable pattern of relationships.
Correspondences
—symbolic analogies and affinities existing among everything in the universe of the
same or similar vibration, in that what affects one thing affects others through this symbolic link.
This is summarized in the Hermetic axiom, “As Above, So Below.”
Creative Imagination
—a technique using a waking fantasy and mental imagery, art or tarot for
working with the unconscious and getting in touch with archetypes.
Dialog
—a conversation between two or more people or, in the imagination, between aspects of self or
divinity involving an exchange of ideas or opinions. As a creative technique, its purpose is usually
for insight.
Divination
—looking for answers, usually regarding the future or hidden knowledge by supernatural or
Divine means, usually through a tool of symbolic communication like tarot.
Duality
—a series of core opposites that lie at the center of our human experience and that of all life on

--- Page 284 ---
Earth: light/dark, sun/moon, day/night, masculine/feminine, logos/eros, active/passive,
introvert/extravert, attraction/repulsion, conscious/unconscious, good/evil, cold/hot, wet/dry. Some
of these may be combined or divided into Quaternities that, for Jung, represented wholeness.
Ego
—the center of consciousness that acts as an organizing and synthesizing activity within the
psyche serving to motivate or decide on actions. The seat of volition and part of the personality
that one consciously recognizes as I or me.
Element(s)
—what, in western culture, were believed to be the four basic constituents of the natural
world: fire, water, air, and earth. The four suits of the Minor Arcana are most often equated with
these four elements in the following ways:
Emotion(s)
—the psychological response to a symbol. An involuntary mental state arising
spontaneously and accompanied by physiological changes; a feeling. They are impulses to
action; what moves us by acting as motivators of plans and decisions.
Enantiodromia
—the reversal of a psychic situation. The turning of things over into their own opposite,
or the tendency of seemingly polar extremes to flip over into one another.
Eros
—originally a Greek word for passionate love, it is one of two opposed life principles that express
an essential mind/heart or rational/emotional opposition. Jung saw Eros as a desire for psychic
relatedness that he associated with the feminine and the 
anima
 in a man.
Extravert
—someone stimulated primarily by the outer world, objects and actions rather than inner
thoughts and emotions. (see Introvert)
Fate
—assumes that an outcome is the result or consequence of an earlier, though sometimes
unknown, cause including the playing out of previous actions or tendencies as they've become
conditioning and habits. Jung noted, “That which we do not confront in ourselves, we will meet as
fate.”
Functions
—Jung's typology consisting of four different fundamental ways of perceiving and
interpreting reality. Sensation tells you that something exists (through the senses); Thinking tells
you what it is (its definition); Feeling tells you whether it is agreeable or not (its value); Intuition
tells you whence it comes and where it is going (its possibilities). They are often related to the
four elements and four suits of the tarot Minor Arcana. (see Elements)
Hero Archetype
—an archetypal figure that ideally presents a model of an ego functioning in accord
with the Self.
Hero's Journey
—the “archetypal quest scenario” in the individuation process of a person and that can
be found in a great many myths and stories, as devised by Joseph Campbell.
Hierosgamos
—mystic marriage of opposites, ritually between the gods; a term used in alchemy and
also by Jung to describe the conjunction and union of archetypal figures that occurs during the
individuation process. (see Individuation)
Imagination
—images formed in the mind, not bound by the rules of time and space, that have a
symbolic or metaphysical reality in the psyche rather than a physical one. Jung believed that
one's own imagination should be the guiding star in working with the unconscious.
Individuation
—the journey or process of becoming one's unique self. It involves the quest for self-
knowledge and the process of personality development that includes withdrawing projections
from others to become a self-aware human being. It leads to the fullest possible actualization of
an individual, the expansion of the boundaries of what one can experience and the ability to make
conscious choices.
Inferior/Superior Functions
 (see Functions and Rational/Irrational Functions)
Inflation
—over-identification with a superhuman archetypal figure resulting in a distorted and
exaggerated sense of self that is often disastrous. (see Ego Inflation)
Introvert
—someone stimulated primarily by a reflective nature whose first awareness is of inner
reactions to objects and events, causing this person to think and consider before acting. (see

--- Page 285 ---
Extravert)
Intuition
—looking within; perception or comprehension without the use of rational processes. One of
the four Jungian Functions. (see Function)
Jung/Jungian
—Carl Gustav Jung (1875-1961), a Swiss psychologist and psychiatrist. Refers to
Jung's theories of psychology, which have greatly influenced the exploration of psyche or soul
and the interpretation of symbols. Subsequent commentators have expanded greatly on his core
ideas.
Libido
—desires or impulses consisting of psychic energy; the natural, unimpeded urge of life. A lack
of libido is experienced as depression. (see Psychic Energy)
Logos
—originally from the Greek meaning “rationality,” it is one of two nominally opposed life
principles that express an essential mind/heart or rational/emotional opposition. Jung came to see
it as reason or “objective interest,” which he associated with the masculine and the animus in
women. (see Eros, Duality)
Major Arcana
—the twenty-one numbered cards and one unnumbered card, within the seventy-eight
cards of the tarot, that serve as a map outlining the journey toward self-realization. Arcana comes
from a root word meaning “box or chest” and an older root meaning “to contain or guard.” The
Arcana of the tarot can thus be seen as magical secrets that are contained or guarded.
Mandala
—a circular geometric diagram, often based on a four-fold pattern, used in Eastern
philosophy as an aid to meditation and appearing in dreams and visions as spontaneous attempts
on the part of the unconscious to create order.
Marseilles Tarot
—a woodcut-based deck that was produced as early as the seventeenth century,
becoming the standard divination deck in France and other parts of Europe. The Minor Arcana
number cards show only suit markers (no pictorial scenes). Also called “Tarot de Marseille.”
Masculine-Feminine Duality
—positive and negative poles of energy, whose dynamic interaction
propagates, motivates and illuminates our lives. These two life principles in all men, all women,
and all of nature retain a sexual overtone that is a paradigm for the realization of the opposites
and their ultimate transcendence. More and more we've come to realize that culturally-assumed
characteristics and roles do not always match the gender-identity of an individual. (see also
Anima/Animus, Eros, Logos, Yang/Yin)
Mask
—an outward persona that conceals some or all of one's true personality. Often unaware of our
masks, they are adopted without conscious choice. (see Persona)
Meaning
—the felt sense or inner import of a thing or experience; the projective identification of
emotions. For Jung it is a human instinct that serves as the prime mover through whose impetus
all other aspects of the psyche, including ego consciousness itself, are brought to birth. It is the
product of lived relationships in the world and helps us endure what would otherwise be
unendurable.
Minor Arcana
—fifty-six cards out of the seventy-eight card tarot deck, divided into four Suits, each
comprising ten cards, numbered from Ace through Ten, and four Court Cards. In modern
interpretation they usually refer to the events, situations, or people encountered. (see Elements)
Myth
—a story, real or fictional, that appeals to the consciousness of a people by embodying its
cultural ideals and/or giving expression to deep, commonly felt emotions. Arising from the
collective unconscious and encoded in the human brain, myths express archetypal themes that
are part of the human species as a whole.
Numinous/Numinosity
—the quality of a thing or event that generates a deep emotional response of
awe, fascination, and mystery in those who behold or experience it; supernatural.
Persona
—the public guise or mask with which you present yourself to the world, acting as a buffer
between our secret selves and the world. The bridge from the ego to the outer world.
Projection
—an unconscious, autonomous process whereby we first see in the persons, objects, and
happenings in our environment those tendencies, characteristics, potentials, and shortcomings
that really belong to us. Simultaneously, we tend to deny or reject these qualities in ourselves.
Projections, when reclaimed, are useful tools toward gaining self-knowledge.
Psyche
—from a Greek word meaning “breath” and hence “soul” or “spirit,” signifying the processes of
the mind, both conscious and unconscious, that make up the personality. Psychology is the study
of the psyche as mind. The human psyche, like the human body, is not static but a process of

--- Page 286 ---
evolving toward expanded awareness.
Psychic Energy
—a raw energy sourced in the archetypes and animal instincts of humans that
originally focuses on self-gratification but is developed and refined into more mature phases of
consciousness.
Puer/Puella
—a Latin term for “youth,” used by Jungians to signify the archetype of the “eternal child”
(or 
Puer Aeternus
). A Puer is the man who never becomes emotionally mature and Puella, the
woman. These imply adolescent playfulness, beauty, charisma, and allure, but there is an
inability to fully commit in a responsible way.
Quaternity
—a structured grouping of four things that represent a whole, like the four elements, suits,
Jungian functions.
Rational/Irrational Functions
—Jung further classifies his four functions as either rational or irrational.
Thinking and feeling involve discrimination (or judging) among values and choices based on
rational thought or evaluative feeling; intuition and sensation involve spontaneous perceiving,
either through the senses (things as they are) or via extra-sensory perceptions (what others may
not see). Whichever function is superior in your personality, its opposite is your inferior or least
developed function; i.e., if you are primarily Intuitive then the mutually exclusive Sensate is your
least developed function. (see Functions)
Rider-Waite-Smith Tarot
—refers to the “Rider-Waite” deck, first published in 1909, designed by
Arthur Edward Waite and art by Pamela Colman Smith. As the first deck to have representational
scenes on all cards it has become the most influential of modern decks.
Ritual
—symbols in action. Symbolic behavior, consciously performed in which the purpose is
transformation.
Self
— the all-encompassing center of the entire psyche. As the most complete expression of one's
individuality, it is a guiding force and center of expanded awareness and stability, including both
conscious and unconscious. It is responsible for the process of individuation.
Shadow
—an aspect of the unconscious self as it appears in dreams, fantasies, and outer reality that
embodies qualities in ourselves which we can't or prefer not to see. Unrecognized by the
conscious self, it is repressed, denied, and unactualized. We tend to project these seemingly
negative qualities and sometimes positive potentials onto someone else. A major part of the
individuation process involves bringing shadow material into conscious awareness and
integrating those qualities within the self. In addition to the personal shadow there is a collective
shadow (the devil) and a cultural shadow (like racism) that may be projected onto a whole group
of people.
Spread
—an arrangement of cards used for a tarot reading. The different positions in a spread
(sometimes called a layout) usually carry different meanings, and each card is interpreted
according to the position where it appears. (see Tarot Reading)
Symbol
—something that represents something else by association, resemblance, or convention,
especially a material object used to represent something invisible and which inspires feelings and
intuitions. Its meaning transcends all specifics and includes many seeming opposites. The
unconscious communicates by means of symbols.
Synchronicity
—a theory developed by Jung and the physicist Wolfgang Pauli of an acausal
orderedness to things that happen at a singular moment in time. Often called a meaningful
coincidence, Nichols deems it a coincidence between internal states and external events. When
synchronicities occur it signals that an archetypal power has been activated. It is an opportunity
for the consciousness and unconscious to become related.
Tarot Reading
—tarot cards dealt into a given pattern or spread, then studied in regard to their
symbolism and relationship to one another in order to find an answer to a question. (see Spread)
Tarot
—card deck of 78 cards: 22 Trumps or Majors; 56 Minors in four suits of ten numbered (pip)
cards and four court cards in each suit. Also, a trick-taking game played with such a deck.
Temenos
—a sacred ground, precinct, or courtyard, usually enclosed. In Jungian psychology it implies
an environment that is safe for deep transformative work, where something dark and hidden can
be brought forth into the light, or where the divine breaks through into human awareness.
Transcendent Function
—the mediating activity of the symbol that incorporates and points beyond
the contradictory positions of opposites like the consciousness and the unconscious. Through it, it

--- Page 287 ---
becomes possible for the entire psychic totality, the Self, to come into consciousness.
Transformation
—a marked change, usually for the better. It often reveals conditioned attitudes and
responses that aren't working so as to replace them with more authentic, spontaneous
experiences.
Unconscious
—the hidden part of the mind or unknown inner world, which is believed to house
memories of forgotten events, as well as archetypal images from ancient times. The unconscious
is usually inaccessible, even to the individual without the use of processes such as dreams,
hypnosis or meditation that can bring memories to the surface.
Uroboros
—Nichols calls it “Tail Eater, that mythical snake who creates, feeds on, and transforms
himself by swallowing his tail. His circular form stands for the original state of unconscious nature,
the primeval womb before the creation of the opposites and for the state of wholeness, the union
of the opposites, desired at the end of the journey.”
Visualization
—(see Active Imagination)
Wise Old Man
—an archetypal figure, representing wisdom and infallibility. Can be a guide or teacher
along the way. (see Senex)
Yang/Yin
—Chinese terms and image for all forms of duality, especially dark/light, wet/dry, cold/hot,
night/day, receptive/active, feminine/masculine. Depicted as equal black and white segments in a
circle in which each part contains a dot of its opposite color. Together they represent wholeness.
Often used as a genderless substitute to replace the terms masculine and feminine when
referring to cards and symbols. Yang is the masculine, creative principle with the powers light,
bright, active, penetrating, and is associated with heaven and spirit. Yin is the feminine aspect of
the godhead with the powers dark, moist, receptive and is associated with earth and soul. (see
Eros and Logos, Masculine-Feminine Duality).

--- Page 288 ---
